.navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Atheism_sidebar" title="Template:Atheism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Atheism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Atheism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Atheism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Atheism</b>, in the broadest sense, is an absence of <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a> in the existence of <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyc-unbelief-def-issues_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyc-unbelief-def-issues-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxdicphil_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxdicphil-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religioustolerance_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-religioustolerance-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Less broadly, atheism is a rejection of the belief that any deities exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb2011-atheism_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-eb2011-atheism-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyc-philosophy_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyc-philosophy-6">[6]</a></sup> In an even narrower sense, atheism is specifically the position that there <em>are</em> no deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyc-unbelief-def-issues_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-encyc-unbelief-def-issues-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxdicphil_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-oxdicphil-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoweRoutledge_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-RoweRoutledge-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Atheism is contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reldef_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-reldef-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED-theism_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-theism-10">[10]</a></sup> which in its most general form is the belief that <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">at least one deity exists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED-theism_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-theism-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymological</a> root for the word <i>atheism</i> originated before the 5th century BCE from the ancient Greek <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%84%CE%B8%CE%B5%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:ἄθεος">ἄθεος</a></span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">atheos</i></span>), meaning "without god(s)". In antiquity, it had multiple uses as a pejorative term applied to those thought to reject the gods worshiped by the larger society,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drachmann_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-drachmann-13">[13]</a></sup> those who were forsaken by the gods, or those who had no commitment to belief in the gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Battling_Whitmarsh_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Battling_Whitmarsh-14">[14]</a></sup> The term denoted a social category created by orthodox religionists into which those who did not share their religious beliefs were placed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Battling_Whitmarsh_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Battling_Whitmarsh-14">[14]</a></sup> The actual term <i>atheism</i> emerged first in the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunter_ch_1_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hunter_ch_1-15">[15]</a></sup> With the spread of <a href="/wiki/Freethought" title="Freethought">freethought</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skeptical_inquiry" title="Skeptical inquiry">skeptical inquiry</a>, and subsequent increase in <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_religion" title="Criticism of religion">criticism of religion</a>, application of the term narrowed in scope. The <a href="/wiki/List_of_atheist_philosophers" title="List of atheist philosophers">first individuals</a> to identify themselves using the word <i>atheist</i> lived in the 18th century during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong1999_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong1999-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunter_ch_1_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hunter_ch_1-15">[15]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, noted for its "unprecedented atheism", witnessed the first significant political movement in history to advocate for the supremacy of human <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HancockLambert_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-HancockLambert-17">[17]</a></sup> In 1967, Albania declared itself the first <a href="/wiki/State_atheism" title="State atheism">official atheist country</a> according to its policy of <a href="/wiki/State_socialism" title="State socialism">state Marxism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arguments for atheism range from philosophical to social and historical approaches. Rationales for not believing in deities include the lack of <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God#Empirical_arguments" title="Existence of God">evidence</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-logical_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-logical-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">problem of evil</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argument_from_inconsistent_revelations" title="Argument from inconsistent revelations">argument from inconsistent revelations</a>, the rejection of concepts that cannot <a href="/wiki/Falsifiability" title="Falsifiability">be falsified</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Argument_from_nonbelief" title="Argument from nonbelief">argument from nonbelief</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-logical_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-logical-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drange-1996_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drange-1996-21">[21]</a></sup> Nonbelievers contend that atheism is a more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parsimonious" title="Parsimonious">parsimonious</a> position than theism and that everyone is born without beliefs in deities;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyc-unbelief-def-issues_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-encyc-unbelief-def-issues-1">[1]</a></sup> therefore, they argue that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophic_burden_of_proof" title="Philosophic burden of proof">burden of proof</a> lies not on the atheist to disprove the existence of gods but on the theist to provide a rationale for theism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Although some atheists have adopted <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secular</a> philosophies (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Secular_humanism" title="Secular humanism">secular humanism</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-honderich_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-honderich-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> there is no ideology or code of conduct to which all atheists adhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since conceptions of atheism vary, accurate estimations of current <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_atheism" title="Demographics of atheism">numbers of atheists</a> are difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin2007_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin2007-26">[26]</a></sup> According to global <a href="/wiki/WIN/GIA" title="WIN/GIA">Win-Gallup International</a> studies, 13% of respondents were "convinced atheists" in 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WIN/GIA_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-WIN/GIA-27">[27]</a></sup> 11% were "convinced atheists" in 2015,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wingia2_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-wingia2-28">[28]</a></sup> and in 2017, 9% were "convinced atheists".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WINGIA_2017_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-WINGIA_2017-29">[29]</a></sup> However, other researchers have advised caution with WIN/Gallup figures since other surveys which have used the same wording for decades and have a bigger sample size have consistently reached lower figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demographics_Oxford_Keysar_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Demographics_Oxford_Keysar-30">[30]</a></sup> An older survey by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="British Broadcasting Corporation">British Broadcasting Corporation</a> (BBC) in 2004 recorded atheists as comprising 8% of the world's population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-2004-demographics_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-2004-demographics-31">[31]</a></sup> Other older estimates have indicated that atheists comprise 2% of the world's population, while the <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">irreligious</a> add a further 12%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb2007-demographics_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-eb2007-demographics-32">[32]</a></sup> According to these polls, Europe and East Asia are the regions with the highest rates of atheism. In 2015, 61% of people in China reported that <a href="/wiki/Irreligion_in_China" title="Irreligion in China">they were atheists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GallupInternational_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-GallupInternational-33">[33]</a></sup> The figures for a 2010 <a href="/wiki/Eurobarometer" title="Eurobarometer">Eurobarometer</a> survey in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU) reported that 20% of the EU population claimed not to believe in "any sort of spirit, God or life force", with France (40%) and Sweden (34%) representing the highest values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-EU-34">[34]</a></sup> According to the 2017 WIN Gallup poll, China and Sweden were the most atheistic countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_and_types">Definitions and types</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AtheismImplicitExplicit3.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="977" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/AtheismImplicitExplicit3.svg/220px-AtheismImplicitExplicit3.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/AtheismImplicitExplicit3.svg/330px-AtheismImplicitExplicit3.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/AtheismImplicitExplicit3.svg/440px-AtheismImplicitExplicit3.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AtheismImplicitExplicit3.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A diagram showing the relationship between the definitions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_and_strong_atheism" title="Weak and strong atheism">weak/strong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Implicit_and_explicit_atheism" title="Implicit and explicit atheism">implicit/explicit</a> atheism. <br/> Explicit strong/positive/hard atheists (in <span style="color:purple;"><b>purple</b></span> on the <b>right</b>) assert that <i>"at least one deity exists"</i> is a false statement. <br/> Explicit weak/negative/soft atheists (in <span style="color:blue;"><b>blue</b></span> on the <b>right</b>) reject or eschew belief that any deities exist without actually asserting that <i>"at least one deity exists"</i> is a false statement. <br/> Implicit weak/negative atheists (in <span style="color:blue;"><b>blue</b></span> on the <b>left</b>), according to authors such as George H. Smith, would include people (such as young children and some agnostics) who do not believe in a deity but have not explicitly rejected such belief. <br/> (Sizes in the diagram are not meant to indicate relative sizes within a population.)</div></div></div>
<p>Writers disagree on how best to define and classify <i>atheism</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb1911-atheism_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-eb1911-atheism-36">[36]</a></sup> contesting what supernatural entities are considered gods, whether atheism is a philosophic position in its own right or merely the absence of one, and whether it requires a conscious, explicit rejection. However the norm is to define atheism in terms of an explicit stance against theism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Atheism has been regarded as compatible with <a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">agnosticism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agnosticism-compatible_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-agnosticism-compatible-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyc-unbelief-compatible_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyc-unbelief-compatible-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-martin-agnosticism-entails_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-martin-agnosticism-entails-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barker-agnostic-atheism_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-barker-agnostic-atheism-43">[43]</a></sup> but has also been contrasted with it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb2011-atheism-critique_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-eb2011-atheism-critique-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb2011concise-atheism_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-eb2011concise-atheism-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb1911-atheism-sceptical_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-eb1911-atheism-sceptical-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Range">Range</span></h3>
<p>Some of the ambiguity and controversy involved in defining <i>atheism</i> arises from difficulty in reaching a consensus for the definitions of words like <i>deity</i> and <i>god</i>. The variety of wildly different <a href="/wiki/Conceptions_of_God" title="Conceptions of God">conceptions of God</a> and deities lead to differing ideas regarding atheism's applicability. The ancient Romans accused Christians of being atheists for not worshiping the <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagan</a> deities. Gradually, this view fell into disfavor as <i>theism</i> came to be understood as encompassing belief in any divinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin2006_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin2006-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>With respect to the range of phenomena being rejected, atheism may counter anything from the existence of a deity, to the existence of any <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">transcendental</a> concepts, such as those of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb2011-Rejection-of-all-religious-beliefs_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-eb2011-Rejection-of-all-religious-beliefs-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Implicit_vs._explicit">Implicit vs. explicit</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Implicit_and_explicit_atheism" title="Implicit and explicit atheism">Implicit and explicit atheism</a></div>
<p>Definitions of atheism also vary in the degree of consideration a person must put to the idea of gods to be considered an atheist. Atheism is commonly defined as the simple absence of belief that any deities exist. This broad definition would include newborns and other people who have not been exposed to theistic ideas. As far back as 1772, <a href="/wiki/Baron_d%27Holbach" title="Baron d'Holbach">Baron d'Holbach</a> said that "All children are born Atheists; they have no idea of God."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
Similarly, <a href="/wiki/George_H._Smith" title="George H. Smith">George H. Smith</a> suggested that: "The man who is unacquainted with theism is an atheist because he does not believe in a god. This category would also include the child with the conceptual capacity to grasp the issues involved, but who is still unaware of those issues. The fact that this child does not believe in god qualifies him as an atheist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> <i>Implicit atheism</i> is "the absence of theistic belief without a conscious rejection of it" and <i>explicit atheism</i> is the conscious rejection of belief.
For the purposes of his paper on "philosophical atheism", <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Nagel" title="Ernest Nagel">Ernest Nagel</a> contested including the mere absence of theistic belief as a type of atheism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nagel1959_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel1959-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Graham_Oppy" title="Graham Oppy">Graham Oppy</a> classifies as <i>innocents</i> those who never considered the question because they lack any understanding of what a god is. According to Oppy, these could be <a href="/wiki/Infant_cognitive_development" title="Infant cognitive development">one-month-old babies</a>, humans with severe traumatic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_injury" title="Brain injury">brain injuries</a>, or patients with <a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">advanced dementia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOppy20184_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOppy20184-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Positive_vs._negative">Positive vs. negative</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Negative_and_positive_atheism" title="Negative and positive atheism">Negative and positive atheism</a></div>
<p>Philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Antony_Flew" title="Antony Flew">Antony Flew</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-presumption_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-presumption-53">[53]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Martin_(philosopher)" title="Michael Martin (philosopher)">Michael Martin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin2006_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin2006-47">[47]</a></sup> have contrasted positive (strong/hard) atheism with negative (weak/soft) atheism. Positive atheism is the explicit affirmation that gods do not exist. Negative atheism includes all other forms of non-theism. According to this categorization, anyone who is not a theist is either a negative or a positive atheist.
The terms <i>weak</i> and <i>strong</i> are relatively recent, while the terms <i>negative</i> and <i>positive</i> atheism are of older origin, having been used (in slightly different ways) in the philosophical literature<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-presumption_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-presumption-53">[53]</a></sup> and in Catholic apologetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>While Martin, for example, asserts that agnosticism <a href="/wiki/Logical_consequence" title="Logical consequence">entails</a> negative atheism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-martin-agnosticism-entails_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-martin-agnosticism-entails-42">[42]</a></sup> many agnostics see their view as distinct from atheism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenny2006_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kenny2006-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> which they may consider no more justified than theism or requiring an equal conviction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenny2006_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kenny2006-55">[55]</a></sup>
The assertion of unattainability of knowledge for or against the existence of gods is sometimes seen as an indication that atheism requires a <a href="/wiki/Leap_of_faith" title="Leap of faith">leap of faith</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
Common atheist responses to this argument include that unproven <i><a href="/wiki/Faith#Religious_views" title="Faith">religious</a></i> propositions deserve as much disbelief as all <i>other</i> unproven propositions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
and that the unprovability of a god's existence does not imply an equal probability of either possibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
Australian philosopher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.J.C._Smart" title="J.J.C. Smart">J.J.C. Smart</a> even argues that "sometimes a person who is really an atheist may describe herself, even passionately, as an agnostic because of unreasonable generalized <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">philosophical skepticism</a> which would preclude us from saying that we know anything whatever, except perhaps the truths of mathematics and formal logic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanford_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-61">[61]</a></sup>
Consequently, some atheist authors, such as <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a>, prefer distinguishing theist, agnostic, and atheist positions along a <a href="/wiki/Spectrum_of_theistic_probability" title="Spectrum of theistic probability">spectrum of theistic probability</a>—the likelihood that each assigns to the statement "God exists".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDawkins200650_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDawkins200650-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition_as_impossible_or_impermanent">Definition as impossible or impermanent</span></h3>
<p>Before the 18th century, the existence of God was so accepted in the Western world that even the possibility of true atheism was questioned. This is called <i>theistic <a href="/wiki/Innatism" title="Innatism">innatism</a></i>—the notion that all people believe in God from birth; within this view was the connotation that atheists are simply in denial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> There is also a position claiming that atheists are quick to believe in God in times of crisis, that atheists make <a href="/wiki/Deathbed_conversion" title="Deathbed conversion">deathbed conversions</a>, or that "<a href="/wiki/There_are_no_atheists_in_foxholes" title="There are no atheists in foxholes">there are no atheists in foxholes</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> There have, however, been examples to the contrary, among them examples of literal "atheists in foxholes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Some atheists have challenged the need for the term "atheism". In his book <i><a href="/wiki/Letter_to_a_Christian_Nation" title="Letter to a Christian Nation">Letter to a Christian Nation</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam_Harris_(author)" title="Sam Harris (author)">Sam Harris</a> wrote:
</p>
<blockquote><p>In fact, "atheism" is a term that should not even exist. No one ever needs to identify himself as a "non-<a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrologer</a>" or a "non-<a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemist</a>". We do not have words for people who doubt that Elvis is still alive or that aliens have traversed the galaxy only to molest ranchers and their cattle. Atheism is nothing more than the noises reasonable people make in the presence of unjustified religious beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris200651_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris200651-66">[66]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ephesians_2,12_-_Greek_atheos.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="135" data-file-width="320" decoding="async" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Ephesians_2%2C12_-_Greek_atheos.jpg/220px-Ephesians_2%2C12_-_Greek_atheos.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/ba/Ephesians_2%2C12_-_Greek_atheos.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ephesians_2,12_-_Greek_atheos.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Greek word <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">αθεοι</span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">atheoi</i></span>), as it appears in the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Ephesians" title="Epistle to the Ephesians">Epistle to the Ephesians</a> 2:12<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> on the early 3rd-century <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_46" title="Papyrus 46">Papyrus 46</a>. It is usually translated into English as "[those who are] without God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[a]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In early <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">ancient Greek</a>, the adjective <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">átheos</i></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%84%CE%B8%CE%B5%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:ἄθεος">ἄθεος</a></span></span>, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Privative_a" title="Privative a">privative ἀ-</a> + <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B8%CE%B5%CF%8C%CF%82" title="wikt:θεός">θεός</a></span></span> "god") meant "godless". It was first used as a term of censure roughly meaning "ungodly" or "impious". In the 5th century BCE, the word began to indicate more deliberate and active godlessness in the sense of "severing relations with the gods" or "denying the gods". The term <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%80%CF%83%CE%B5%CE%B2%CE%AE%CF%82" title="wikt:ἀσεβής">ἀσεβής</a></span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">asebēs</i></span>) then came to be applied against those who impiously denied or disrespected the local gods, even if they believed in other gods. Modern translations of classical texts sometimes render <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">átheos</i></span> as "atheistic". As an abstract noun, there was also <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%80%CE%B8%CE%B5%CF%8C%CF%84%CE%B7%CF%82" title="wikt:ἀθεότης">ἀθεότης</a></span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">atheotēs</i></span>), "atheism". <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> transliterated the Greek word into the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/atheos#Latin" title="wikt:atheos">átheos</a></i></span>. The term found frequent use in the debate between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early Christians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">Hellenists</a>, with each side attributing it, in the pejorative sense, to the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drachmann_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-drachmann-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>atheist</i> (from the French <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ath%C3%A9e" title="wikt:athée">athée</a></i></span>), in the sense of "one who ... denies the existence of God or gods",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> predates <i>atheism</i> in English, being first found as early as 1566,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> and again in 1571.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> <i>Atheist</i> as a label of practical godlessness was used at least as early as 1577.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>atheism</i> was derived from the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ath%C3%A9isme" title="wikt:athéisme">athéisme</a></i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw-online_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-mw-online-74">[73]</a></sup> and appears in English about 1587.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golding_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Golding-75">[74]</a></sup> An earlier work, from about 1534, used the term <i>atheonism</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Related words emerged later: <i>deist</i> in 1621,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1621Deist_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-1621Deist-78">[77]</a></sup> <i>theist</i> in 1662,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a></i> in 1675,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1675Deism_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-1675Deism-80">[79]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theism</a></i> in 1678.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Deism</i> and <i>theism</i> changed meanings slightly around 1700 due to the influence of <i>atheism</i>; <i>deism</i> was originally used as a synonym for today's <i>theism</i> but came to denote a separate philosophical doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Karen_Armstrong" title="Karen Armstrong">Karen Armstrong</a> writes that "During the 16th and 17th centuries, the word 'atheist' was still reserved exclusively for <a href="/wiki/Polemic" title="Polemic">polemic</a> ... The term 'atheist' was an insult. Nobody would have dreamed of calling <em>himself</em> an atheist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong1999_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong1999-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Atheism</i> was first used to describe a self-avowed belief in late 18th-century Europe, specifically denoting disbelief in the <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abrahamic_god" title="Abrahamic god">Abrahamic god</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[b]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a> contributed to the expansion of the term to refer to disbelief in all deities, though it remains common in Western society to describe atheism as simply "disbelief in God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin2006_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin2006-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arguments">Arguments</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:D%27Holbach.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1194" data-file-width="758" decoding="async" height="347" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/D%27Holbach.jpg/220px-D%27Holbach.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/D%27Holbach.jpg/330px-D%27Holbach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/D%27Holbach.jpg/440px-D%27Holbach.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:D%27Holbach.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Baron_d%27Holbach" title="Baron d'Holbach">Paul Henri Thiry, Baron d'Holbach</a>, an 18th-century advocate of atheism.<div class="paragraphbreak" style="margin-top:0.5em"></div>"The source of man's unhappiness is his ignorance of Nature. The pertinacity with which he clings to blind opinions imbibed in his infancy, which interweave themselves with his existence, the consequent prejudice that warps his mind, that prevents its expansion, that renders him the slave of fiction, appears to doom him to continual error."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemological_arguments">Epistemological arguments</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Agnostic_atheism" title="Agnostic atheism">Agnostic atheism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theological_noncognitivism" title="Theological noncognitivism">Theological noncognitivism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Skepticism" title="Skepticism">Skepticism</a>, based on the ideas of <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, asserts that certainty about anything is impossible, so one can never know for sure whether or not a god exists. Hume, however, held that such unobservable metaphysical concepts should be rejected as "sophistry and illusion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hume-metaphysics_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-hume-metaphysics-85">[83]</a></sup> The allocation of agnosticism to atheism is disputed; it can also be regarded as an independent, basic worldview.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zdybicka-p20_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zdybicka-p20-86">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are three main conditions of epistemology: truth, belief and justification. <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lou_Martin" title="Michael Lou Martin">Michael Martin</a> argues that atheism is a justified and rational true belief, but offers no extended epistemological justification because current theories are in a state of controversy. Martin instead argues for "mid-level principles of justification that are in accord with our ordinary and scientific rational practice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michael_Martin_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Martin-87">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other arguments for atheism that can be classified as epistemological or <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontological</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Ignosticism" title="Ignosticism">ignosticism</a>, assert the meaninglessness or unintelligibility of basic terms such as "God" and statements such as "God is all-powerful." <a href="/wiki/Theological_noncognitivism" title="Theological noncognitivism">Theological noncognitivism</a> holds that the statement "God exists" does not express a proposition, but is nonsensical or cognitively meaningless. It has been argued both ways as to whether such individuals can be classified into some form of atheism or agnosticism. Philosophers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_Ayer" title="Alfred Ayer">A. J. Ayer</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodore_M._Drange" title="Theodore M. Drange">Theodore M. Drange</a> reject both categories, stating that both camps accept "God exists" as a proposition; they instead place noncognitivism in its own category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysical_arguments">Metaphysical arguments</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">Monism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">Physicalism</a></div>
<p>Philosopher, <a href="/wiki/Zofia_Zdybicka" title="Zofia Zdybicka">Zofia Zdybicka</a> writes:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Metaphysical atheism ... includes all doctrines that hold to metaphysical monism (the homogeneity of reality). Metaphysical atheism may be either: a) absolute — an explicit denial of God's existence associated with materialistic monism (all materialistic trends, both in ancient and modern times); b) relative — the implicit denial of God in all philosophies that, while they accept the existence of an absolute, conceive of the absolute as not possessing any of the attributes proper to God: transcendence, a personal character or unity. Relative atheism is associated with idealistic monism (pantheism, panentheism, deism).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zdybicka_2005_19_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zdybicka_2005_19-90">[88]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Epikouros_BM_1843.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3800" data-file-width="2533" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Epikouros_BM_1843.jpg/220px-Epikouros_BM_1843.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Epikouros_BM_1843.jpg/330px-Epikouros_BM_1843.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Epikouros_BM_1843.jpg/440px-Epikouros_BM_1843.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Epikouros_BM_1843.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a> is credited with first expounding the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">problem of evil</a>. <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> in his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dialogues_concerning_Natural_Religion" title="Dialogues concerning Natural Religion">Dialogues concerning Natural Religion</a></i> (1779) cited Epicurus in stating the argument as a series of questions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHume1779_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHume1779-91">[89]</a></sup> "Is God willing to prevent evil, but not able? Then he is impotent. Is he able, but not willing? Then he is malevolent. Is he both able and willing? Then whence cometh evil? Is he neither able nor willing? Then why call him God?"</div></div></div>
<p>Most atheists lean toward metaphysical monism: the belief that there is only one kind of ultimate substance. Historically, metaphysical monism took the form of philosophical <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a>, the view that matter formed the basis of all reality; this naturally omitted the possibility of a non-material divine being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham_Oppy_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graham_Oppy-92">[90]</a></sup> Describing the world as "basically matter" in the twenty-first century would be contrary to modern physics, so it is generally seen as an older term that is sometimes mistakenly used interchangeably with <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">physicalism</a>. Physicalism can incorporate the non-matter based physical phenomena, such as light and energy, into its view that only physical entities with physical powers exist, and that science defines and explains what those are.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham_Oppy_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graham_Oppy-92">[90]</a></sup> Physicalism is a monistic ontology: one ultimate substance exists, and it exists as a physical reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniel_Stoljar_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel_Stoljar-93">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Physicalism opposes dualism (the view that there's physical substance and separate mental activities): there is no such thing as a soul, or any other abstract object (such as a mind or a self) that exists independently of physicality. It also opposes neutral monism, which holds to one kind of substance for the universe but makes no claim about its nature, holding to the view that the physical and the mental are both just differing kinds of the same fundamental substance that is in itself neither mental nor physical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leopold_Stubenberg_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leopold_Stubenberg-94">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zdybicka_2005_19_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zdybicka_2005_19-90">[88]</a></sup> Physicalism also opposes <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealism</a> (the view that everything known is based on human mental perception).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D._Gene_Witmer_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-D._Gene_Witmer-95">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">Naturalism</a> is also used by atheists to describe the metaphysical view that everything that exists is fundamentally natural, and that there are no supernatural phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham_Oppy_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-Graham_Oppy-92">[90]</a></sup> Naturalism focuses on how science can explain the world fully with physical laws and through natural phenomena. It's about the idea that the universe is a closed system. Naturalism can be interpreted to allow for a dualist ontology of the mental and physical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_Papineau_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-David_Papineau-96">[94]</a></sup> Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Graham_Oppy" title="Graham Oppy">Graham Oppy</a> references a PhilPapers survey that says 56.5% of philosophers in academics lean toward physicalism; 49.8% lean toward naturalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bourget_and_Chalmers_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bourget_and_Chalmers-97">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Graham Oppy, direct arguments for atheism aim at showing theism fails on its own terms, while indirect arguments are those inferred from direct arguments in favor of something else that is inconsistent with theism. For example, Oppy says arguing for naturalism is an argument for atheism since naturalism and theism "cannot both be true".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruse_and_Bullivant_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruse_and_Bullivant-98">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 53">: 53 </span></sup> Fiona Ellis says that while Oppy's view is common, it is dependent on a narrow view of naturalism. She describes the "expansive naturalism" of <a href="/wiki/John_McDowell" title="John McDowell">John McDowell</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Griffin_(philosopher)" title="James Griffin (philosopher)">James Griffin</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Wiggins" title="David Wiggins">David Wiggins</a> as giving "due respect to scientific findings" while also asserting there are things in human experience which cannot be explained in such terms, such as the concept of value, leaving room for theism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fiona_Ellis_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fiona_Ellis-99">[97]</a></sup> Christopher C. Knight asserts a <a href="/wiki/Theistic_naturalism" title="Theistic naturalism">theistic naturalism</a> that relies on what he terms an "incarnational naturalism" (the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Immanence" title="Immanence">immanence</a>) and does not require any special mode of divine action that would put it outside nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christopher_C._Knight_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christopher_C._Knight-100">[98]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Oppy argues that a strong naturalism favors atheism, though he finds the best direct arguments against theism to be the evidential problem of evil, and arguments concerning the contradictory nature of God were He to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruse_and_Bullivant_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ruse_and_Bullivant-98">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 55–60">: 55–60 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Logical_arguments">Logical arguments</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God#Arguments_against_the_existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">Arguments against the existence of God</a>, <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">Problem of evil</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Argument_from_nonbelief" title="Argument from nonbelief">Divine hiddenness</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seshin_Vasubandhu_Kofukuji.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1018" data-file-width="740" decoding="async" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Seshin_Vasubandhu_Kofukuji.jpg/220px-Seshin_Vasubandhu_Kofukuji.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Seshin_Vasubandhu_Kofukuji.jpg/330px-Seshin_Vasubandhu_Kofukuji.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Seshin_Vasubandhu_Kofukuji.jpg/440px-Seshin_Vasubandhu_Kofukuji.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seshin_Vasubandhu_Kofukuji.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A statue of the Buddhist philosopher Vasubandhu, 1208, <a href="/wiki/K%C5%8Dfuku-ji" title="Kōfuku-ji">Kofukuji Temple</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nara,_Nara" title="Nara, Nara">Nara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a></div></div></div>
<p>Some atheists hold the view that the various <a href="/wiki/Conceptions_of_God" title="Conceptions of God">conceptions of gods</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Personal_god" title="Personal god">personal god</a> of Christianity, are ascribed logically inconsistent qualities. Such atheists present <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God#Logical_arguments" title="Existence of God">deductive arguments</a> against the existence of God, which assert the incompatibility between certain traits, such as perfection, creator-status, <a href="/wiki/Immutability_(theology)" title="Immutability (theology)">immutability</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">omniscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omnipresence" title="Omnipresence">omnipresence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">omnipotence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omnibenevolence" title="Omnibenevolence">omnibenevolence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(philosophy)" title="Transcendence (philosophy)">transcendence</a>, personhood (a personal being), non-physicality, <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">justice</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mercy" title="Mercy">mercy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-logical_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-logical-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Theodicy" title="Theodicy">Theodicean</a> atheists believe that the world as they experience it cannot be reconciled with the qualities commonly ascribed to God and gods by theologians. They argue that an <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">omniscient</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">omnipotent</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Omnibenevolence" title="Omnibenevolence">omnibenevolent</a> God is not compatible with a world where there is <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">evil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suffering" title="Suffering">suffering</a>, and where divine love is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine_hiddenness" title="Divine hiddenness">hidden</a> from many people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drange-1996_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Drange-1996-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>
A similar argument is attributed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siddhartha_Gautama" title="Siddhartha Gautama">Siddhartha Gautama</a>, the founder of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup> The medieval <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_philosophy" title="Buddhist philosophy">Buddhist philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/Vasubandhu" title="Vasubandhu">Vasubandhu</a> (4/5th century) who outlined <a href="/wiki/Creator_in_Buddhism" title="Creator in Buddhism">numerous Buddhist arguments against God</a>, wrote in his <i>Sheath of <a href="/wiki/Abhidharma" title="Abhidharma">Abhidharma</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abhidharmako%C5%9Bak%C4%81rik%C4%81" title="Abhidharmakośakārikā">Abhidharmakosha</a>):</i></p><blockquote><p>Besides, do you say that God finds joy in seeing the creatures which he has created in the prey of all the distress of existence, including the tortures of the hells? Homage to this kind of God! The profane stanza expresses it well: "One calls him Rudra because he burns, because he is sharp, fierce, redoubtable, an eater of flesh, blood and marrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reductionary_accounts_of_religion">Reductionary accounts of religion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_origin_of_religions" title="Evolutionary origin of religions">Evolutionary origin of religions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_psychology_of_religion" title="Evolutionary psychology of religion">Evolutionary psychology of religion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Psychology_of_religion" title="Psychology of religion">Psychology of religion</a></div>
<p>Philosopher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach" title="Ludwig Andreas Feuerbach">Ludwig Feuerbach</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup>
and psychoanalyst <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> have argued that God and other religious beliefs are human inventions, created to fulfill various psychological and emotional wants or needs, or a projection mechanism from the 'Id' omnipotence; for <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a>, in 'Materialism and Empirio-criticism', against the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Machism" title="Russian Machism">Russian Machism</a>, the followers of <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Mach" title="Ernst Mach">Ernst Mach</a>, Feuerbach was the final argument against belief in a god. This is also a view of many <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a>, influenced by the work of Feuerbach, argued that belief in God and religion are social functions, used by those in power to oppress the working class. According to <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Bakunin" title="Mikhail Bakunin">Mikhail Bakunin</a>, "the idea of God implies the abdication of human reason and justice; it is the most decisive negation of human liberty, and necessarily ends in the enslavement of mankind, in theory, and practice." He reversed <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>'s aphorism that if God did not exist, it would be necessary to invent him, writing instead that "if God really existed, it would be necessary to abolish him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Atheism.2C_religions_and_spirituality"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Atheism,_religions_and_spirituality">Atheism, religions and spirituality</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nontheistic_religions" title="Nontheistic religions">Nontheistic religions</a></div>
<p>Atheism is not mutually exclusive with respect to some religious and spiritual belief systems, including <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syntheism" title="Syntheism">Syntheism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ra%C3%ABlism" title="Raëlism">Raëlism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paganism_(contemporary_religions)" title="Paganism (contemporary religions)">Neopagan</a> <a href="/wiki/New_religious_movement" title="New religious movement">movements</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neopaganism_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neopaganism-107">[105]</a></sup>
such as <a href="/wiki/Wicca" title="Wicca">Wicca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wicca_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wicca-108">[106]</a></sup>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C4%80stika" title="Āstika">Āstika</a> schools in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheism_in_Hinduism" title="Atheism in Hinduism">Hinduism</a> hold atheism to be a valid path to <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a>, but extremely difficult, for the atheist cannot expect any help from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gods_in_Hinduism" title="Gods in Hinduism">the divine</a> on their journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup>
Jainism believes the universe is eternal and has no need for a creator deity, however <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tirthankaras" title="Tirthankaras">Tirthankaras</a> are revered beings who can transcend space and time<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-devdutt_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-devdutt-110">[108]</a></sup> and have more power than the god <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Secular_Buddhism" title="Secular Buddhism">Secular Buddhism</a> does not advocate belief in gods. Early Buddhism was atheistic as <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a>'s path involved no mention of gods. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/God_in_Buddhism" title="God in Buddhism">Later conceptions</a> of Buddhism consider Buddha himself a god, suggest adherents can attain godhood, and revere <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">Bodhisattvas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atheism_and_negative_theology">Atheism and negative theology</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Apophatic_theology#Apophatic_theology_and_atheism" title="Apophatic theology">Atheism and negative theology</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Apophatic_theology" title="Apophatic theology">Apophatic theology</a> is often assessed as being a version of atheism or agnosticism, since it cannot say truly that God exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup> "The comparison is crude, however, for conventional atheism treats the existence of God as a predicate that can be denied ("God is nonexistent"), whereas negative theology denies that God has predicates".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> "God or the Divine is" without being able to attribute qualities about "what He is" would be the prerequisite of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Positive_theology" title="Positive theology">positive theology</a> in negative theology that distinguishes theism from atheism. "Negative theology is a complement to, not the enemy of, positive theology".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Atheistic_philosophies">Atheistic philosophies</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheist_existentialism" title="Atheist existentialism">Atheist existentialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secular_humanism" title="Secular humanism">Secular humanism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Axiology" title="Axiology">Axiological</a>, or constructive, atheism rejects the existence of gods in favor of a "higher absolute", such as <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">humanity</a>. This form of atheism favors humanity as the absolute source of ethics and values, and permits individuals to resolve moral problems without resorting to God. Marx and Freud used this argument to convey messages of liberation, full-development, and unfettered happiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zdybicka-p20_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zdybicka-p20-86">[84]</a></sup> One of the most common <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_atheism" title="Criticism of atheism">criticisms of atheism</a> has been to the contrary: that denying the existence of a god either leads to <a href="/wiki/Moral_relativism" title="Moral relativism">moral relativism</a> and leaves one with no moral or ethical foundation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-misconceptions_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-misconceptions-116">[114]</a></sup> or renders life <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meaning_of_life_(religious)" title="Meaning of life (religious)">meaningless</a> and miserable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">Blaise Pascal</a> argued this view in his <i><a href="/wiki/Pens%C3%A9es" title="Pensées">Pensées</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre" title="Jean-Paul Sartre">Jean-Paul Sartre</a> identified himself as a representative of an "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheist_existentialism" title="Atheist existentialism">atheist existentialism</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESartre2004127_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartre2004127-119">[117]</a></sup>
concerned less with denying the existence of God than with establishing that "man needs ... to find himself again and to understand that nothing can save him from himself, not even a valid proof of the existence of God."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESartre200145_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartre200145-120">[118]</a></sup>
Sartre said a corollary of his atheism was that "if God does not exist, there is at least one being in whom existence precedes essence, a being who exists before he can be defined by any concept, and ... this being is man."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESartre2004127_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartre2004127-119">[117]</a></sup>
Sartre described the practical consequence of this atheism as meaning that there are no <i>a priori</i> rules or absolute values that can be invoked to govern human conduct, and that humans are "condemned" to invent these for themselves, making "man" absolutely "responsible for everything he does".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESartre200132_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartre200132-121">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion_and_morality">Religion and morality</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Atheism_and_religion" title="Atheism and religion">Atheism and religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_atheism" title="Criticism of atheism">Criticism of atheism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Secular_ethics" title="Secular ethics">Secular ethics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Secular_morality" title="Secular morality">Secular morality</a></div>
<p>Joseph Baker and Buster Smith assert that one of the common themes of atheism is that most atheists "typically construe atheism as more moral than religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker_and_Smith_2015_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baker_and_Smith_2015-122">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Association_with_world_views_and_social_behaviors">Association with world views and social behaviors</span></h3>
<p>Sociologist <a href="/wiki/Phil_Zuckerman" title="Phil Zuckerman">Phil Zuckerman</a> analyzed previous social science research on secularity and non-belief and concluded that societal well-being is positively correlated with irreligion. He found that there are much lower concentrations of atheism and secularity in poorer, less developed nations (particularly in Africa and South America) than in the richer industrialized democracies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup>
His findings relating specifically to atheism in the US were that compared to religious people in the US, "atheists and secular people" are less <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalistic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prejudice" title="Prejudice">prejudiced</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">antisemitic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogmatic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethnocentrism" title="Ethnocentrism">ethnocentric</a>, closed-minded, and authoritarian, and in US states with the highest percentages of atheists, the murder rate is lower than average. In the most religious states, the murder rate is higher than average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zuckerman_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zuckerman-125">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-126">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irreligion">Irreligion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">Irreligion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lightmatter_buddha3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="320" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Lightmatter_buddha3.jpg/170px-Lightmatter_buddha3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Lightmatter_buddha3.jpg/255px-Lightmatter_buddha3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/05/Lightmatter_buddha3.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lightmatter_buddha3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> is sometimes described as nontheistic because of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/God_in_Buddhism" title="God in Buddhism">the absence</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creator_god" title="Creator god">creator god</a>, but that can be too simplistic a view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>People who self-identify as atheists are often assumed to be <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">irreligious</a>, but some sects within major religions reject the existence of a personal, <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">creator deity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winston2_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-winston2-129">[127]</a></sup>
In recent years, certain religious denominations have accumulated a number of openly atheistic followers, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheist_Jew" title="Atheist Jew">atheistic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Humanistic_Judaism" title="Humanistic Judaism">humanistic Judaism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup>
and <a href="/wiki/Christian_atheism" title="Christian atheism">Christian atheists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup> The strictest sense of positive atheism does not entail any specific beliefs outside of disbelief in any deity; as such, atheists can hold any number of spiritual beliefs. For the same reason, atheists can hold a wide variety of ethical beliefs, ranging from the <a href="/wiki/Moral_universalism" title="Moral universalism">moral universalism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a>, which holds that a moral code should be applied consistently to all humans, to <a href="/wiki/Moral_nihilism" title="Moral nihilism">moral nihilism</a>, which holds that morality is meaningless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> Atheism is accepted as a valid philosophical position within some varieties of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup> Philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Alain_de_Botton" title="Alain de Botton">Alain de Botton</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Bard" title="Alexander Bard">Alexander Bard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jan_S%C3%B6derqvist" title="Jan Söderqvist">Jan Söderqvist</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> have argued that atheists should reclaim useful components of religion in secular society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Divine_command">Divine command</span></h3>
<p>According to Plato's <a href="/wiki/Euthyphro_dilemma" title="Euthyphro dilemma">Euthyphro dilemma</a>, the role of the gods in determining right from wrong is either unnecessary or arbitrary. <a href="/wiki/Divine_command_theory" title="Divine command theory">The argument that morality must be derived from God</a>, and cannot exist without a wise creator, has been a persistent feature of political if not so much philosophical debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kant_CPR_A811_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kant_CPR_A811-142">[140]</a></sup>
Moral precepts such as "murder is wrong" are seen as <a href="/wiki/Divine_law" title="Divine law">divine laws</a>, requiring a divine lawmaker and judge. However, many atheists argue that treating morality legalistically involves a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/False_analogy" title="False analogy">false analogy</a>, and that morality does not depend on a lawmaker in the same way that laws do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a> believed in a morality independent of theistic belief, and stated that morality based upon God "has truth only if God is truth—it stands or falls with faith in God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fortin_&amp;_Benestad_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fortin_&amp;_Benestad-144">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craig_&amp;_Moreland_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Craig_&amp;_Moreland-145">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-146">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>There exist <a href="/wiki/Normative_ethics" title="Normative ethics">normative ethical systems</a> that do not require principles and rules to be given by a deity. Some include <a href="/wiki/Virtue_ethics" title="Virtue ethics">virtue ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kantian_ethics" title="Kantian ethics">Kantian ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Objectivism" title="Objectivism">Objectivism</a>. Sam Harris has proposed that moral prescription (ethical rule making) is not just an issue to be explored by philosophy, but that we can meaningfully practice a <a href="/wiki/Science_of_morality" title="Science of morality">science of morality</a>. Any such scientific system must, nevertheless, respond to the criticism embodied in the <a href="/wiki/Naturalistic_fallacy" title="Naturalistic fallacy">naturalistic fallacy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>Philosophers <a href="/wiki/Susan_Neiman" title="Susan Neiman">Susan Neiman</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup>
and <a href="/wiki/Julian_Baggini" title="Julian Baggini">Julian Baggini</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[147]</a></sup>
(among others) assert that behaving ethically only because of a divine mandate is not true ethical behavior but merely blind obedience. Baggini argues that atheism is a superior basis for ethics, claiming that a moral basis external to religious imperatives is necessary to evaluate the morality of the imperatives themselves—to be able to discern, for example, that "thou shalt steal" is immoral even if one's religion instructs it—and that atheists, therefore, have the advantage of being more inclined to make such evaluations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup>
The contemporary British political philosopher <a href="/wiki/Martin_Cohen_(philosopher)" title="Martin Cohen (philosopher)">Martin Cohen</a> has offered the more historically telling example of Biblical injunctions in favor of torture and slavery as evidence of how religious injunctions follow political and social customs, rather than vice versa, but also noted that the same tendency seems to be true of supposedly dispassionate and objective philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup> Cohen extends this argument in more detail in <i>Political Philosophy from Plato to Mao</i>, where he argues that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a> played a role in perpetuating social codes from the early 7th century despite changes in secular society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_of_religion">Criticism of religion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_religion" title="Criticism of religion">Criticism of religion</a></div>
<p>Some prominent atheists—most recently <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hitchens" title="Christopher Hitchens">Christopher Hitchens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam_Harris_(author)" title="Sam Harris (author)">Sam Harris</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a>, and following such thinkers as <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_G._Ingersoll" title="Robert G. Ingersoll">Robert G. Ingersoll</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>, and novelist <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Saramago" title="José Saramago">José Saramago</a>—have criticized religions, citing harmful aspects of religious practices and doctrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karl_Marx.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Karl_Marx.jpg/170px-Karl_Marx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Karl_Marx.jpg/255px-Karl_Marx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Karl_Marx.jpg/340px-Karl_Marx.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karl_Marx.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a></div></div></div>
<p>The 19th-century German political theorist and sociologist Karl Marx called religion "the sigh of the oppressed creature, the heart of a heartless world, and the soul of soulless conditions. It is the <a href="/wiki/Opium_of_the_people" title="Opium of the people">opium of the people</a>". He goes on to say, "The abolition of religion as the illusory happiness of the people is the demand for their real happiness. To call on them to give up their illusions about their condition is to call on them to give up a condition that requires illusions. The criticism of religion is, therefore, in embryo, the criticism of that vale of tears of which religion is the halo."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Lenin</a> said that "every religious idea and every idea of God is unutterable vileness ... of the most dangerous kind, 'contagion' of the most abominable kind. Millions of sins, filthy deeds, acts of violence and physical contagions ... are far less dangerous than the subtle, spiritual idea of God decked out in the smartest ideological costumes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MartinAmis_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-MartinAmis-155">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sam Harris criticizes Western religion's reliance on divine authority as lending itself to <a href="/wiki/Authoritarianism" title="Authoritarianism">authoritarianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogmatism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris2006a_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris2006a-156">[154]</a></sup>
There is a correlation between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_fundamentalism" title="Religious fundamentalism">religious fundamentalism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrinsic_religious_orientation" title="Extrinsic religious orientation">extrinsic religion</a> (when religion is held because it serves ulterior interests)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moreira-almeida2006_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moreira-almeida2006-157">[155]</a></sup> and authoritarianism, dogmatism, and prejudice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[156]</a></sup>
These arguments—combined with historical events that are argued to demonstrate the dangers of religion, such as the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inquisition" title="Inquisition">inquisitions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Witch-hunt" title="Witch-hunt">witch trials</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Religious_terrorism" title="Religious terrorism">terrorist attacks</a>—have been used in response to claims of beneficial effects of belief in religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[157]</a></sup>
Believers counter-argue that some <a href="/wiki/State_atheism" title="State atheism">regimes that espouse atheism</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, have also been guilty of mass murder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_S._Feinberg,_Paul_D._Feinberg_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_S._Feinberg,_Paul_D._Feinberg-160">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Totalitarianism_and_Atheism_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Totalitarianism_and_Atheism-161">[159]</a></sup> In response to those claims, atheists such as Sam Harris and Richard Dawkins have stated that Stalin's atrocities were influenced not by atheism but by dogmatic <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, and that while Stalin and Mao happened to be atheists, they did not do their deeds in the name of atheism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDawkins2006291_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDawkins2006291-162">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_atheism" title="History of atheism">History of atheism</a></div>
<p>While the earliest-found usage of the term <i>atheism</i> is in 16th-century <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw-online_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-mw-online-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Golding_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Golding-75">[74]</a></sup> ideas that would be recognized today as atheistic are documented from the <a href="/wiki/Vedic_period" title="Vedic period">Vedic period</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pandian_1996_64_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pandian_1996_64-164">[162]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis-165">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Indian_religions">Early Indian religions</span></h3>
