<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheism_in_Hinduism" title="Atheism in Hinduism">Atheism in Hinduism</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023981488">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .rquote{width:auto!important;float:none!important}}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote rquote" style="float: right; width: 33%;"><p>Who really knows? <br/>Who will here proclaim it? <br/>Whence was it produced? Whence is this creation? <br/>The gods came afterwards, with the creation of this universe. <br/>Who then knows whence it has arisen?</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Nasadiya_Sukta" title="Nasadiya Sukta">Nasadiya Sukta</a>, concerning the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_of_the_universe" title="Origin of the universe">origin of the universe</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rig_Veda" title="Rig Veda">Rig Veda</a>, <i>10:129-6</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kramer1986_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kramer1986-166">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christian2011_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christian2011-167">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2008_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2008-168">[166]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Atheistic schools are found in early Indian thought and have existed from the times of the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion" title="Historical Vedic religion">historical Vedic religion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pandian_1996_64_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pandian_1996_64-164">[162]</a></sup>
Among the six <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astika_and_Nastika" title="Astika and Nastika">orthodox</a> schools of Hindu philosophy, <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a>, the oldest philosophical school of thought, does not accept God, and the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mimamsa" title="Mimamsa">Mimamsa</a> also rejected the notion of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[167]</a></sup>
The thoroughly materialistic and anti-theistic philosophical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%C4%81rv%C4%81ka" title="Cārvāka">Cārvāka</a> (or <i>Lokāyata</i>) school that originated in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> around the 6th century BCE is probably the most explicitly atheistic school of philosophy in India, similar to the Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyrenaic_school" title="Cyrenaic school">Cyrenaic school</a>. This branch of Indian philosophy is classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nastika" title="Nastika">heterodox</a> due to its rejection of the authority of <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> and hence is not considered part of the six orthodox schools of <a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Indian philosophy</a>. It is noteworthy as evidence of a materialistic movement in ancient India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Chatterjee and Datta explain that our understanding of Cārvāka philosophy is fragmentary, based largely on criticism of the ideas by other schools, and that it is not a living tradition:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[170]</a></sup><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Though <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a> in some form or other has always been present in India, and occasional references are found in the Vedas, the Buddhistic literature, the Epics, as well as in the later philosophical works we do not find any systematic work on materialism, nor any organized school of followers as the other philosophical schools possess. But almost every work of the other schools states, for refutation, the materialistic views. Our knowledge of Indian materialism is chiefly based on these.</p></blockquote>
<p>Other Indian philosophies generally regarded as atheistic include <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Classical Samkhya</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mimamsa" title="Mimamsa">Purva Mimamsa</a>. The rejection of a personal creator "God" is also seen in <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joshi_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joshi-173">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_antiquity">Classical antiquity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1023981488" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote rquote" style="float: right; width: 33%;"><p>Does then anyone say there are gods in heaven? There are not, there are not, if a man is willing not to give foolish credence to the ancient story. Consider for yourselves, don't form an opinion on the basis of my words!</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Bellerophon" title="Bellerophon">Bellerophon</a> denying the existence of the gods, from <a href="/wiki/Euripides" title="Euripides">Euripides</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Bellerophon_(play)" title="Bellerophon (play)">Bellerophon</a></i> <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 5th century BCE, fr. 286 <i>TrGF</i> 1-5<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coll_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coll-174">[172]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Western atheism has its roots in <a href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic_philosophy" title="Pre-Socratic philosophy">pre-Socratic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" title="Greek philosophy">Greek philosophy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis-165">[163]</a></sup> but atheism in the modern sense was extremely rare in ancient Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winiarczyk_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Winiarczyk-176">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis_165-2"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis-165">[163]</a></sup> Pre-Socratic <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">Atomists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> attempted to explain the world in a purely <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialistic</a> way and interpreted religion as a human reaction to natural phenomena,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burkert1985_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985-177">[175]</a></sup> but did not explicitly deny the gods' existence, and some scholarship has recognised a rational theology in his thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burkert1985_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985-177">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vassa_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vassa-178">[176]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anaxagoras" title="Anaxagoras">Anaxagoras</a>, whom <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a> calls "the atheist",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[177]</a></sup> was accused of impiety and condemned for stating that "the sun is a type of incandescent stone", an affirmation with which he tried to deny the divinity of the celestial bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[178]</a></sup> In the late fifth century BCE, the Greek <a href="/wiki/Lyric_poetry" title="Lyric poetry">lyric poet</a> <a href="/wiki/Diagoras_of_Melos" title="Diagoras of Melos">Diagoras of Melos</a> was sentenced to death in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> under the charge of being a "godless person" (ἄθεος) after he made fun of the <a href="/wiki/Eleusinian_Mysteries" title="Eleusinian Mysteries">Eleusinian Mysteries</a>, but he fled the city to escape punishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winiarczyk_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-Winiarczyk-176">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burkert1985_177-2"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985-177">[175]</a></sup> In post-classical antiquity, philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sextus_Empiricus" title="Sextus Empiricus">Sextus Empiricus</a> described Diagoras as an "atheist" who categorically denied the existence of the gods,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIC_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIC-181">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SextEmp_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-SextEmp-182">[180]</a></sup> but in modern scholarship Marek Winiarczyk has defended the view that Diagoras was not an atheist in the modern sense, in a view that has proved influential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winiarczyk_176-2"><a href="#cite_note-Winiarczyk-176">[174]</a></sup> On the other hand, the verdict has been challenged by <a href="/wiki/Tim_Whitmarsh" title="Tim Whitmarsh">Tim Whitmarsh</a>, who argues that Diagoras rejected the gods on the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">problem of evil</a>, and this argument was in turn alluded to in Euripides' fragmentary play <i><a href="/wiki/Bellerophon_(play)" title="Bellerophon (play)">Bellerophon</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whit_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whit-183">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sisyphus_fragment" title="Sisyphus fragment">A fragment</a> from a lost Attic drama that featured <a href="/wiki/Sisyphus" title="Sisyphus">Sisyphus</a>, which has been attributed to both <a href="/wiki/Critias" title="Critias">Critias</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euripides" title="Euripides">Euripides</a>, claims that a clever man invented "the fear of the gods" in order to frighten people into behaving morally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davi_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davi-184">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winiarczyk_176-3"><a href="#cite_note-Winiarczyk-176">[174]</a></sup>
Sceptical statements about popular religion have also been attributed to the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Prodicus" title="Prodicus">Prodicus</a>: <a href="/wiki/Philodemus" title="Philodemus">Philodemus</a> reports that Prodicus believed that "the gods of popular belief do not exist nor does he know them, but primitive man, [out of admiration, deified] the fruits of the earth and virtually everything that contributed to his existence". More recent scholarship has reappraised the evidence in <a href="/wiki/Philodemus" title="Philodemus">Philodemus</a> and concluded that <a href="/wiki/Prodicus" title="Prodicus">Prodicus</a> defended his own philosophical theology against popular religious belief, rather than radical atheism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vass_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vass-186">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koul_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koul-187">[185]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Protagoras" title="Protagoras">Protagoras</a> has sometimes been taken to be an atheist, but rather espoused agnostic views, commenting that "Concerning the gods I am unable to discover whether they exist or not, or what they are like in form; for there are many hindrances to knowledge, the obscurity of the subject and the brevity of human life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garland2008_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garland2008-189">[187]</a></sup> The Athenian public associated Socrates (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 470</span>–399 BCE) with the trends in pre-Socratic philosophy towards naturalistic inquiry and the rejection of divine explanations for phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burkert1985_177-3"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985-177">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bremmer_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bremmer-190">[188]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aristophanes" title="Aristophanes">Aristophanes</a>' comic play <i><a href="/wiki/The_Clouds" title="The Clouds">The Clouds</a></i> (performed 423 BCE) portrays Socrates as teaching his students that the traditional Greek deities do not exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burkert1985_177-4"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985-177">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bremmer_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bremmer-190">[188]</a></sup> Socrates was later tried and executed under the charge of not believing in the gods of the state and instead worshipping foreign gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burkert1985_177-5"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985-177">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bremmer_190-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bremmer-190">[188]</a></sup> Socrates himself vehemently denied the charges of atheism at his trial<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burkert1985_177-6"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985-177">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bremmer_190-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bremmer-190">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[189]</a></sup> and all the surviving sources about him indicate that he was a very devout man, who prayed to the rising sun and believed that the <a href="/wiki/Pythia" title="Pythia">oracle at Delphi</a> spoke the word of <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burkert1985_177-7"><a href="#cite_note-Burkert1985-177">[175]</a></sup> From a survey of these 5th-century BCE philosophers, <a href="/wiki/David_Sedley" title="David Sedley">David Sedley</a> has concluded that none of them openly defended radical atheism, but since Classical sources clearly attest to radical atheist ideas Athens probably had an "atheist underground".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sedl_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sedl-192">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>Religious scepticism continued into the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a>, and from this period the most important Greek thinker in the development of atheism was the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 300 BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis_165-3"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis-165">[163]</a></sup> Drawing on the ideas of Democritus and the Atomists, he espoused a materialistic philosophy according to which the universe was governed by the laws of chance without the need for divine intervention (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_determinism" title="Scientific determinism">scientific determinism</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EpicStanEncycl_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-EpicStanEncycl-193">[191]</a></sup> Although Epicurus still maintained that the gods existed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hickson2014_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hickson2014-194">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GraftonMostSettis_165-4"><a href="#cite_note-GraftonMostSettis-165">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EpicStanEncycl_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-EpicStanEncycl-193">[191]</a></sup> he believed that they were uninterested in human affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EpicStanEncycl_193-2"><a href="#cite_note-EpicStanEncycl-193">[191]</a></sup> The aim of the Epicureans was to attain <i><a href="/wiki/Ataraxia" title="Ataraxia">ataraxia</a></i> ("peace of mind") and one important way of doing this was by exposing fear of divine wrath as irrational. The Epicureans also denied the existence of an afterlife and the need to fear divine punishment after death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EpicStanEncycl_193-3"><a href="#cite_note-EpicStanEncycl-193">[191]</a></sup> A slightly later contemporary to <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Euhemerus" title="Euhemerus">Euhemerus</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 300 BCE) published his view that the gods were only the deified rulers, conquerors and founders of the past, and that their cults and religions were in essence the continuation of vanished kingdoms and earlier political structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[193]</a></sup> Although not strictly an atheist, Euhemerus was later criticized for having "spread atheism over the whole inhabited earth by obliterating the gods".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[194]</a></sup> In the 3rd-century BCE, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a> philosophers <a href="/wiki/Theodorus_the_Atheist" title="Theodorus the Atheist">Theodorus Cyrenaicus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIC_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIC-181">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[195]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Strato_of_Lampsacus" title="Strato of Lampsacus">Strato of Lampsacus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[196]</a></sup> were also reputed to deny the existence of the gods.
</p><p>Sceptical ideas concerning the gods continued into the <a href="/wiki/Second_Sophistic" title="Second Sophistic">Second Sophistic</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhonism" title="Pyrrhonism">Pyrrhonist</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Sextus_Empiricus" title="Sextus Empiricus">Sextus Empiricus</a> (approx. 160-210 CE)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[197]</a></sup> compiled a large number of ancient arguments against the existence of gods, recommending that one should <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epoche" title="Epoche">suspend judgment</a> regarding the matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[198]</a></sup> His relatively large volume of surviving works had a lasting influence on later philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gordonstein_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-gordonstein-201">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>The meaning of "atheist" changed over the course of classical antiquity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winiarczyk_176-4"><a href="#cite_note-Winiarczyk-176">[174]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christians</a> were widely reviled as "atheists" because they did not believe in the existence of the Graeco-Roman deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE1913_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-CE1913-202">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winiarczyk_176-5"><a href="#cite_note-Winiarczyk-176">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferguson1993_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson1993-203">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherwin_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherwin-204">[202]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, Christians were executed for their rejection of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_deities" title="List of Roman deities">Roman gods</a> in general and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_cult_of_ancient_Rome" title="Imperial cult of ancient Rome">Imperial cult of ancient Rome</a> in particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherwin_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sherwin-204">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maycock_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maycock-205">[203]</a></sup> There was, however, a heavy struggle between Christians and pagans, in which each group accused the other of atheism, for not practicing the religion which they considered correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duran_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duran-206">[204]</a></sup> When Christianity became the state religion of Rome under <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> in 381, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_heresy" title="Christian heresy">heresy</a> became a punishable offense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maycock_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-Maycock-205">[203]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Ages_to_the_Renaissance">Early Middle Ages to the Renaissance</span></h3>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_world" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a> experienced a <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Golden Age</a>. Along with advances in science and philosophy, Arab and Persian lands produced rationalists and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freethinkers" title="Freethinkers">freethinkers</a> who were skeptical about <a href="/wiki/Prophecy" title="Prophecy">prophecy</a> and revealed religion, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_al_Warraq" title="Muhammad al Warraq">Muhammad al Warraq</a> (fl. 9th century), <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Rawandi" title="Ibn al-Rawandi">Ibn al-Rawandi</a> (827–911), and <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr_al-Razi" title="Abu Bakr al-Razi">Abu Bakr al-Razi</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 865</span>–925),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[205]</a></sup> as well as outspoken atheists such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Ma%CA%BFarri" title="Al-Maʿarri">al-Maʿarri</a> (973–1058). Al-Ma'arri wrote and taught that religion itself was a "fable invented by the ancients"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholson318_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholson318-208">[206]</a></sup> and that humans were "of two sorts: those with brains, but no religion, and those with religion, but no brains".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[207]</a></sup> Despite the fact that these authors were relatively prolific writers, little of their work survives, mainly being preserved through quotations and excerpts in later works by Muslim <a href="/wiki/Apologetics" title="Apologetics">apologists</a> attempting to refute them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Europe, the espousal of atheistic views was rare during the Early Middle Ages and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Inquisition" title="Medieval Inquisition">Medieval Inquisition</a>); <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a> and theology were the dominant interests pertaining to religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zdybicka_2005_4_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zdybicka_2005_4-211">[209]</a></sup> There were, however, movements within this period that furthered heterodox conceptions of the Christian god, including differing views of the nature, transcendence, and knowability of God. Individuals and groups such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johannes_Scotus_Eriugena" title="Johannes Scotus Eriugena">Johannes Scotus Eriugena</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_of_Dinant" title="David of Dinant">David of Dinant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amalric_of_Bena" title="Amalric of Bena">Amalric of Bena</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Brethren_of_the_Free_Spirit" title="Brethren of the Free Spirit">Brethren of the Free Spirit</a> maintained Christian viewpoints with <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheistic</a> tendencies. <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_of_Cusa" title="Nicholas of Cusa">Nicholas of Cusa</a> held to a form of <a href="/wiki/Fideism" title="Fideism">fideism</a> he called <i><a href="/wiki/De_Docta_Ignorantia" title="De Docta Ignorantia">docta ignorantia</a></i> ("learned ignorance"), asserting that God is beyond human categorization, and thus our knowledge of him is limited to conjecture. <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a> inspired anti-metaphysical tendencies with his <a href="/wiki/Nominalism" title="Nominalism">nominalistic</a> limitation of human knowledge to singular objects, and asserted that the divine <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> could not be intuitively or rationally apprehended by human intellect. Followers of Ockham, such as <a href="/wiki/John_of_Mirecourt" title="John of Mirecourt">John of Mirecourt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_of_Autrecourt" title="Nicholas of Autrecourt">Nicholas of Autrecourt</a> furthered this view. The resulting division between <a href="/wiki/Faith_and_rationality" title="Faith and rationality">faith and reason</a> influenced later radical and reformist theologians such as <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zdybicka_2005_4_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zdybicka_2005_4-211">[209]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> did much to expand the scope of free thought and skeptical inquiry. Individuals such as <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> sought experimentation as a means of explanation, and opposed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Appeal_to_authority" title="Appeal to authority">arguments from religious authority</a>. Other critics of religion and the Church during this time included <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Machiavelli" title="Niccolò Machiavelli">Niccolò Machiavelli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bonaventure_des_P%C3%A9riers" title="Bonaventure des Périers">Bonaventure des Périers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michel_de_Montaigne" title="Michel de Montaigne">Michel de Montaigne</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Rabelais" title="François Rabelais">François Rabelais</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gordonstein_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-gordonstein-201">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_period">Early modern period</span></h3>
<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Blainey" title="Geoffrey Blainey">Geoffrey Blainey</a> wrote that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a> had paved the way for atheists by attacking the authority of the Catholic Church, which in turn "quietly inspired other thinkers to attack the authority of the new Protestant churches".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[210]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">Deism</a> gained influence in France, Prussia, and England. The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a> was "probably the first well known 'semi-atheist' to announce himself in a Christian land in the modern era", according to Blainey. Spinoza believed that natural laws explained the workings of the universe. In 1661, he published his <i>Short Treatise on God</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Christianity" title="Criticism of Christianity">Criticism of Christianity</a> became increasingly frequent in the 17th and 18th centuries, especially in France and England, where there appears to have been a religious <a href="/wiki/Malaise" title="Malaise">malaise</a>, according to contemporary sources. Some Protestant thinkers, such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, espoused a materialist philosophy and skepticism toward supernatural occurrences, while Spinoza rejected <a href="/wiki/Divine_providence" title="Divine providence">divine providence</a> in favor of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panentheistic" title="Panentheistic">panentheistic</a> naturalism. By the late 17th century, deism came to be openly espoused by intellectuals such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Toland_(Irish_Philosopher)" title="John Toland (Irish Philosopher)">John Toland</a> who coined the term "pantheist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first known explicit atheist was the German critic of religion <a href="/wiki/Matthias_Knutzen" title="Matthias Knutzen">Matthias Knutzen</a> in his three writings of 1674.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[213]</a></sup> He was followed by two other explicit atheist writers, the Polish ex-Jesuit philosopher <a href="/wiki/Kazimierz_%C5%81yszczy%C5%84ski" title="Kazimierz Łyszczyński">Kazimierz Łyszczyński</a> and in the 1720s by the French priest <a href="/wiki/Jean_Meslier" title="Jean Meslier">Jean Meslier</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[214]</a></sup> In the course of the 18th century, other openly atheistic thinkers followed, such as <a href="/wiki/Baron_d%27Holbach" title="Baron d'Holbach">Baron d'Holbach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Andr%C3%A9_Naigeon" title="Jacques-André Naigeon">Jacques-André Naigeon</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/French_materialism" title="French materialism">French materialists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holbach-SoN_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holbach-SoN-217">[215]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> in contrast, though an advocate of tolerance, urged authorities not to tolerate atheism, believing that the denial of God's existence would undermine the social order and lead to chaos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[216]</a></sup>
</p><p>The philosopher <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> developed a skeptical epistemology grounded in <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>'s philosophy has strongly questioned the very possibility of metaphysical knowledge. Both philosophers undermined the metaphysical basis of natural theology and criticized classical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arguments_for_the_existence_of_God" title="Arguments for the existence of God">arguments for the existence of God</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2063" data-file-width="1807" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg/170px-Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg/255px-Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg/340px-Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach" title="Ludwig Andreas Feuerbach">Ludwig Feuerbach</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Essence_of_Christianity" title="The Essence of Christianity">The Essence of Christianity</a></i> (1841) would greatly influence philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Engels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Marx</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Strauss" title="David Strauss">David Strauss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Nietzsche</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Max_Stirner" title="Max Stirner">Max Stirner</a>. He considered God to be a human invention and religious activities to be wish-fulfillment. For this he is considered the founding father of modern <a href="/wiki/Anthropology_of_religion" title="Anthropology of religion">anthropology of religion</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Blainey notes that, although <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> is widely considered to have strongly contributed to atheistic thinking during the Revolution, he also considered fear of God to have discouraged further disorder, having said "If God did not exist, it would be necessary to invent him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[217]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Reflections_on_the_Revolution_in_France" title="Reflections on the Revolution in France">Reflections on the Revolution in France</a></i> (1790), the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a> denounced atheism, writing of a "literary cabal" who had "some years ago formed something like a regular plan for the destruction of the Christian religion. This object they pursued with a degree of zeal which hitherto had been discovered only in the propagators of some system of piety ... These atheistical fathers have a bigotry of their own". But, Burke asserted, "man is by his constitution a religious animal" and "atheism is against, not only our reason, but our instincts; and ... it cannot prevail long".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Baron_d%27Holbach" title="Baron d'Holbach">Baron d'Holbach</a> was a prominent figure in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Enlightenment" title="French Enlightenment">French Enlightenment</a> who is best known for his atheism and for his voluminous writings against religion, the most famous of them being <i><a href="/wiki/The_System_of_Nature" title="The System of Nature">The System of Nature</a></i> (1770) but also <i><a href="/wiki/Christianity_Unveiled" title="Christianity Unveiled">Christianity Unveiled</a></i>. One goal of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> was a restructuring and subordination of the clergy with respect to the state through the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Constitution_of_the_Clergy" title="Civil Constitution of the Clergy">Civil Constitution of the Clergy</a>. Attempts to enforce it led to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anticlericalism" title="Anticlericalism">anti-clerical</a> violence and the expulsion of many clerics from France, lasting until the <a href="/wiki/Thermidorian_Reaction" title="Thermidorian Reaction">Thermidorian Reaction</a>. The radical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacobin_Club" title="Jacobin Club">Jacobins</a> seized power in 1793, ushering in the <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Terror" title="Reign of Terror">Reign of Terror</a>. The Jacobins were deists and introduced the <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_the_Supreme_Being" title="Cult of the Supreme Being">Cult of the Supreme Being</a> as a new French state religion. Some atheists surrounding <a href="/wiki/Jacques_H%C3%A9bert" title="Jacques Hébert">Jacques Hébert</a> instead sought to establish a <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_Reason" title="Cult of Reason">Cult of Reason</a>, a form of atheistic pseudo-religion with a goddess personifying reason. The <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_era" title="Napoleonic era">Napoleonic era</a> further institutionalized the secularization of French society.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the latter half of the 19th century, atheism rose to prominence under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalistic</a> and freethinking philosophers. Many prominent German philosophers of this era denied the existence of deities and were critical of religion, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach" title="Ludwig Andreas Feuerbach">Ludwig Feuerbach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Arthur Schopenhauer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Max_Stirner" title="Max Stirner">Max Stirner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1842, <a href="/wiki/George_Holyoake" title="George Holyoake">George Holyoake</a> was the last person imprisoned in Great Britain due to atheist beliefs. <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Law" title="Stephen Law">Stephen Law</a> notes that he may have also been the first imprisoned on such a charge. Law states that Holyoake "first coined the term '<a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secularism</a>'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[221]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_1900">Since 1900</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Marxism_and_religion" title="Marxism and religion">Marxism and religion</a></div>
<p>Atheism, particularly in the form of practical atheism, advanced in many societies in the 20th century. Atheistic thought found recognition in a wide variety of other, broader philosophies, such as <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">existentialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Objectivism" title="Objectivism">Objectivism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Secular_humanism" title="Secular humanism">secular humanism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nihilism" title="Nihilism">nihilism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Logical_positivism" title="Logical positivism">logical positivism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-feminism_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-feminism-224">[222]</a></sup> and the general scientific<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[223]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rationalist_movement" title="Rationalist movement">rationalist movement</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bezbozhnik_u_stanka_22-1929.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2869" data-file-width="2005" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Bezbozhnik_u_stanka_22-1929.jpg/170px-Bezbozhnik_u_stanka_22-1929.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Bezbozhnik_u_stanka_22-1929.jpg/255px-Bezbozhnik_u_stanka_22-1929.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Bezbozhnik_u_stanka_22-1929.jpg/340px-Bezbozhnik_u_stanka_22-1929.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bezbozhnik_u_stanka_22-1929.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1929 cover of the USSR <a href="/wiki/League_of_Militant_Atheists" title="League of Militant Atheists">League of Militant Atheists</a> <a href="/wiki/Bezbozhnik_(magazine)" title="Bezbozhnik (magazine)">magazine</a>, showing the gods of the <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic religions</a> being crushed by the <a href="/wiki/Five-year_plans_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Five-year plans of the Soviet Union">Soviet five-year plan</a></div></div></div>
<p>In addition, <a href="/wiki/State_atheism" title="State atheism">state atheism</a> emerged in Eastern Europe and Asia during that period, particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> under <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[224]</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/China#Communist_Party" title="China">Communist China</a> under <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a>. Atheist and anti-religious policies in the Soviet Union included <a href="/wiki/Soviet_anti-religious_legislation" title="Soviet anti-religious legislation">numerous legislative acts</a>, the outlawing of religious instruction in the schools, and the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Militant_Atheists" title="League of Militant Atheists">League of Militant Atheists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Viking_p.494_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Viking_p.494-228">[226]</a></sup> After Mao, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China#Stance_on_religion" title="Communist Party of China">Chinese Communist Party</a> remains an atheist organization, and regulates, but does not forbid, the practice of religion in mainland China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[229]</a></sup>
</p><p>While Geoffrey Blainey has written that "the most ruthless leaders in the Second World War were atheists and secularists who were intensely hostile to both Judaism and Christianity",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[230]</a></sup> Richard Madsen has pointed out that Hitler and Stalin each opened and closed churches as a matter of political expedience, and Stalin softened his opposition to Christianity in order to improve public acceptance of his regime during the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[231]</a></sup> Blackford and Schüklenk have written that "the Soviet Union was undeniably an atheist state, and the same applies to Maoist China and Pol Pot's fanatical Khmer Rouge regime in Cambodia in the 1970s. That does not, however, show that the atrocities committed by these totalitarian dictatorships were the result of atheist beliefs, carried out in the name of atheism, or caused primarily by the atheistic aspects of the relevant forms of communism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[232]</a></sup>
</p><p>Logical positivism and <a href="/wiki/Scientism" title="Scientism">scientism</a> paved the way for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neopositivism" title="Neopositivism">neopositivism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analytical_philosophy" title="Analytical philosophy">analytical philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structuralism" title="Structuralism">structuralism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">naturalism</a>. Neopositivism and analytical philosophy discarded classical rationalism and metaphysics in favor of strict empiricism and epistemological <a href="/wiki/Nominalism" title="Nominalism">nominalism</a>. Proponents such as <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a> emphatically rejected belief in God. In his early work, <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein" title="Ludwig Wittgenstein">Ludwig Wittgenstein</a> attempted to separate metaphysical and supernatural language from rational discourse. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.J._Ayer" title="A.J. Ayer">A.J. Ayer</a> asserted the unverifiability and meaninglessness of religious statements, citing his adherence to the empirical sciences. Relatedly the <a href="/wiki/Structural_anthropology" title="Structural anthropology">applied structuralism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Claude_L%C3%A9vi-Strauss" title="Claude Lévi-Strauss">Lévi-Strauss</a> sourced religious language to the human subconscious in denying its transcendental meaning. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Niemeyer_Findlay" title="John Niemeyer Findlay">J.N. Findlay</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.J.C._Smart" title="J.J.C. Smart">J.J.C. Smart</a> argued that the existence of God is not logically necessary. Naturalists and materialistic monists such as <a href="/wiki/John_Dewey" title="John Dewey">John Dewey</a> considered the natural world to be the basis of everything, denying the existence of God or immortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanford_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[233]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_developments">Other developments</span></h3>
<p>Other leaders like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periyar_E.V._Ramasamy" title="Periyar E.V. Ramasamy">Periyar E.V. Ramasamy</a>, a prominent atheist leader of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, fought against <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmins" title="Brahmins">Brahmins</a> for discriminating and dividing people in the name of <a href="/wiki/Caste" title="Caste">caste</a> and religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[234]</a></sup> This was highlighted in 1956 when he arranged for the erection of a statue depicting a Hindu god in a humble representation and made <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antitheistic" title="Antitheistic">antitheistic</a> statements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[235]</a></sup>
</p><p>Atheist <a href="/wiki/Vashti_McCollum" title="Vashti McCollum">Vashti McCollum</a> was the plaintiff in a landmark 1948 <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a> case that struck down religious education in US public schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[236]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Madalyn_Murray_O%27Hair" title="Madalyn Murray O'Hair">Madalyn Murray O'Hair</a> was one of the most influential American atheists; she brought forth the 1963 Supreme Court case <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Murray_v._Curlett" title="Murray v. Curlett">Murray v. Curlett</a></i> which banned compulsory prayer in public schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[237]</a></sup> In 1966, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine asked "Is God Dead?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[238]</a></sup> in response to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_of_God_theological_movement" title="Death of God theological movement">Death of God theological movement</a>, citing the estimation that nearly half of all people in the world lived under an anti-religious power, and millions more in Africa, Asia, and South America seemed to lack knowledge of the Christian view of theology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[239]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Freedom_From_Religion_Foundation" title="Freedom From Religion Foundation">Freedom From Religion Foundation</a> was co-founded by Anne Nicol Gaylor and her daughter, <a href="/wiki/Annie_Laurie_Gaylor" title="Annie Laurie Gaylor">Annie Laurie Gaylor</a>, in 1976 in the United States, and incorporated nationally in 1978. It promotes the <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separation of church and state</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aboutCalling_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-aboutCalling-242">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[241]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">Berlin Wall</a>, the number of actively anti-religious regimes has declined considerably. In 2006, Timothy Shah of the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Forum</a> noted "a worldwide trend across all major religious groups, in which God-based and faith-based movements in general are experiencing increasing confidence and influence vis-à-vis secular movements and ideologies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[242]</a></sup>
However, <a href="/wiki/Gregory_S._Paul" title="Gregory S. Paul">Gregory S. Paul</a> and Phil Zuckerman consider this a myth and suggest that the actual situation is much more complex and nuanced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[243]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2010 survey found that those identifying themselves as atheists or agnostics are on average more knowledgeable about religion than followers of major faiths. Nonbelievers scored better on questions about tenets central to Protestant and Catholic faiths. Only Mormon and Jewish faithful scored as well as atheists and agnostics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion_knowledge_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-religion_knowledge-246">[244]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, the first "Women in Secularism" conference was held in Arlington, Virginia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[245]</a></sup> Secular Woman was organized in 2012 as a national organization focused on nonreligious women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[246]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Atheist_feminism" title="Atheist feminism">atheist feminist movement</a> has also become increasingly focused on fighting sexism and <a href="/wiki/Sexual_harassment" title="Sexual harassment">sexual harassment</a> within the atheist movement itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[247]</a></sup>
In August 2012, Jennifer McCreight (the organizer of <a href="/wiki/Boobquake" title="Boobquake">Boobquake</a>) founded a movement within atheism known as Atheism Plus, or A+, that "applies skepticism to everything, including social issues like sexism, racism, politics, poverty, and crime".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[250]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013 the first atheist monument on American government property was unveiled at the <a href="/wiki/Bradford_County,_Florida" title="Bradford County, Florida">Bradford County</a> Courthouse in Florida: a 1,500-pound granite bench and plinth inscribed with quotes by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Madalyn_Murray_O%27Hair" title="Madalyn Murray O'Hair">Madalyn Murray O'Hair</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[253]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Atheism">New Atheism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Atheism" title="New Atheism">New Atheism</a></div>
<p>"New Atheism" is a movement among some early-21st-century atheist writers who have advocated the view that "religion should not simply be tolerated but should be countered, criticized, and exposed by rational argument wherever its influence arises."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[254]</a></sup>
The movement is commonly associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam_Harris_(author)" title="Sam Harris (author)">Sam Harris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">Richard Dawkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victor_J._Stenger" title="Victor J. Stenger">Victor J. Stenger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hitchens" title="Christopher Hitchens">Christopher Hitchens</a>, and to some extent <a href="/wiki/Ayaan_Hirsi_Ali" title="Ayaan Hirsi Ali">Ayaan Hirsi Ali</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[255]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStenger2009_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStenger2009-258">[256]</a></sup> Several best-selling books by these authors, published between 2004 and 2007, form the basis for much of the discussion of "New" Atheism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStenger2009_258-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStenger2009-258">[256]</a></sup> In best-selling books, the religiously-motivated terrorist <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">events of 9/11</a> and the partially successful attempts of the <a href="/wiki/Discovery_Institute" title="Discovery Institute">Discovery Institute</a> to change the American science curriculum to include <a href="/wiki/Creationism" title="Creationism">creationist</a> ideas, together with support for those ideas from <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> in 2005, have been cited by authors such as Harris, Dennett, Dawkins, Stenger, and Hitchens as evidence of a need to move toward a more secular society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sharedvalues_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-sharedvalues-259">[257]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_atheism" title="Demographics of atheism">Demographics of atheism</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Religiosity_and_education" title="Religiosity and education">Religiosity and education</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:552px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Countries_by_percentage_of_Unaffiliated%E2%80%93Pew_Research_2010.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="263" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Countries_by_percentage_of_Unaffiliated%E2%80%93Pew_Research_2010.svg/550px-Countries_by_percentage_of_Unaffiliated%E2%80%93Pew_Research_2010.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Countries_by_percentage_of_Unaffiliated%E2%80%93Pew_Research_2010.svg/825px-Countries_by_percentage_of_Unaffiliated%E2%80%93Pew_Research_2010.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Countries_by_percentage_of_Unaffiliated%E2%80%93Pew_Research_2010.svg/1100px-Countries_by_percentage_of_Unaffiliated%E2%80%93Pew_Research_2010.svg.png 2x" width="550"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Countries_by_percentage_of_Unaffiliated%E2%80%93Pew_Research_2010.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irreligion_by_country" title="Irreligion by country">Nonreligious population by country</a>, 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[258]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>It is difficult to quantify the number of atheists in the world. Respondents to religious-belief polls may define "atheism" differently or draw different distinctions between <i>atheism</i>, non-religious beliefs, and non-theistic religious and spiritual beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[259]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Hindu_atheism" title="Hindu atheism">Hindu atheist</a> would declare oneself as a <a href="/wiki/Hindus" title="Hindus">Hindu</a>, although also being an atheist at the same time. Most of the time this happens because atheism and irreligion are not officially recognised in India. <a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">Apostasy</a> is allowed under the right to <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion_in_India" title="Freedom of religion in India">freedom of religion</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_India" title="Constitution of India">Constitution</a> (but blasphemy is prohibited), there are no specific laws catering to atheists and they are considered as belonging to the religion of their birth for administrative purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[260]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[261]</a></sup> A 2010 survey published in <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a></i> found that the non-religious made up about 9.6% of the world's population, and atheists about 2.0%, with a very large majority based in Asia. This figure did not include those who follow atheistic religions, such as some Buddhists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb-2010_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-eb-2010-264">[262]</a></sup> The average annual change for atheism from 2000 to 2010 was −0.17%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb-2010_264-1"><a href="#cite_note-eb-2010-264">[262]</a></sup> Broad estimates of those who have an absence of belief in a god range from 500 million to 1.1 billion people worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[264]</a></sup> Scholars have indicated that <a href="/wiki/Desecularization" title="Desecularization">global atheism may be in decline</a> as a percentage of the global population due to irreligious countries having the lowest birth rates in the world and religious countries generally having higher birth rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CambridgeZuckerman_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-CambridgeZuckerman-267">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[267]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to global <a href="/wiki/WIN/GIA" title="WIN/GIA">Win-Gallup International</a> studies, 13% of respondents were "convinced atheists" in 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallup2012_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gallup2012-270">[268]</a></sup> 11% were "convinced atheists" in 2015,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wingia2_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-wingia2-28">[28]</a></sup> and in 2017, 9% were "convinced atheists".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WINGIA_2017_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-WINGIA_2017-29">[29]</a></sup> As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Atheism&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the top 10 surveyed countries with people who viewed themselves as "convinced atheists" were <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (47%), <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> (31%), the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a> (30%), <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> (29%), <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> (15%), <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> (15%), <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> (14%), <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> (10%), <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> (10%), <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> (10%), and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a> (10%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[269]</a></sup> A 2012 study by the NORC found that East Germany had the highest percentage of atheists while Czech Republic had the second highest amount.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[270]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_No_Belief_enhanced_2010.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1245" data-file-width="1360" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Europe_No_Belief_enhanced_2010.png/220px-Europe_No_Belief_enhanced_2010.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Europe_No_Belief_enhanced_2010.png/330px-Europe_No_Belief_enhanced_2010.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Europe_No_Belief_enhanced_2010.png/440px-Europe_No_Belief_enhanced_2010.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_No_Belief_enhanced_2010.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage of people in various European countries who said: "I don't believe there is any sort of spirit, God or life force." (2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB2010_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB2010-273">[271]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>According to the 2010 Eurobarometer Poll, the percentage of those polled who agreed with the statement "you don't believe there is any sort of spirit, God or life force" varied from a high percentage in France (40%), Czech Republic (37%), Sweden (34%), Netherlands (30%), and Estonia (29%); medium-high percentage in Germany (27%), Belgium (27%), UK (25%); to very low in Poland (5%), Greece (4%), Cyprus (3%), Malta (2%), and Romania (1%), with the European Union as a whole at 20%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EU_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-EU-34">[34]</a></sup> In a 2012 Eurobarometer poll on discrimination in the European Union, 16% of those polled considered themselves non-believers/agnostics, and 7% considered themselves atheists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[272]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> survey in 2012, about 18% of Europeans are <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">religiously unaffiliated</a>, including agnostics and atheists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Religiously_Unaffiliated_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-Religiously_Unaffiliated-275">[273]</a></sup> According to the same survey, the religiously unaffiliated are the majority of the population only in two European countries: Czech Republic (75%) and Estonia (60%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Religiously_Unaffiliated_275-1"><a href="#cite_note-Religiously_Unaffiliated-275">[273]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span></h3>
<p>There are another three countries, and <a href="/wiki/Special_administrative_regions_of_China" title="Special administrative regions of China">one special administrative region of China</a> or regions where the unaffiliated make up a majority of the population: <a href="/wiki/Irreligion_in_North_Korea" title="Irreligion in North Korea">North Korea</a> (71%), <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Japan#Opposition_to_organised_religion" title="Religion in Japan">Japan</a> (57%), Hong Kong (56%), and China (52%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Religiously_Unaffiliated_275-2"><a href="#cite_note-Religiously_Unaffiliated-275">[273]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australasia">Australasia</span></h3>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Bureau_of_Statistics" title="Australian Bureau of Statistics">Australian Bureau of Statistics</a>, 30% of Australians have "no religion", a category that includes atheists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abs_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-abs-276">[274]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a 2013 census, 42% of <a href="/wiki/New_Zealanders" title="New Zealanders">New Zealanders</a> reported having no religion, up from 30% in 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[275]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h3>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Values_Survey" title="World Values Survey">World Values Survey</a>, 4.4% of Americans self-identified as atheists in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WVS_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-WVS-278">[276]</a></sup> However, the same survey showed that 11.1% of all respondents stated "no" when asked if they believed in God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WVS_278-1"><a href="#cite_note-WVS-278">[276]</a></sup> In 1984, these same figures were 1.1% and 2.2%, respectively. According to a 2014 report by the Pew Research Center, 3.1% of the US adult population identify as atheist, up from 1.6% in 2007; and within the religiously unaffiliated (or "no religion") demographic, atheists made up 13.6%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[277]</a></sup> According to the 2015 General Sociological Survey the number of atheists and agnostics in the US has remained relatively flat in the past 23 years since in 1991 only 2% identified as atheist and 4% identified as agnostic and in 2014 only 3% identified as atheists and 5% identified as agnostics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSS_2014_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-GSS_2014-280">[278]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the American Family Survey, 34% were found to be religiously unaffiliated in 2017 (23% 'nothing in particular', 6% agnostic, 5% atheist).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[279]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[280]</a></sup> According to the Pew Research Center, in 2014, 22.8% of the American population does not identify with a religion, including atheists (3.1%) and agnostics (4%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[281]</a></sup> According to a PRRI survey, 24% of the population is unaffiliated. Atheists and agnostics combined make up about a quarter of this unaffiliated demographic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[282]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_world">Arab world</span></h3>
<p>In recent years, the profile of atheism has risen substantially in the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-285">[283]</a></sup> In major cities across the region, such as <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, atheists have been organizing in cafés and social media, despite regular crackdowns from authoritarian governments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_285-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-285">[283]</a></sup> A 2012 poll by Gallup International revealed that 5% of Saudis considered themselves to be "convinced atheists".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_285-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto-285">[283]</a></sup> However, very few young people in the Arab world have atheists in their circle of friends or acquaintances. According to one study, less than 1% did in Morocco, Egypt, Saudi Arabia, or Jordan; only 3% to 7% in the United Arab Emirates, Bahrain, Kuwait, and Palestine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tabah2016_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tabah2016-286">[284]</a></sup> When asked whether they have "seen or heard traces of atheism in [their] locality, community, and society" only about 3% to 8% responded yes in all the countries surveyed. The only exception was the UAE, with a percentage of 51%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tabah2016_286-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tabah2016-286">[284]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Wealth.2C_education.2C_and_reasoning_style"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wealth,_education,_and_reasoning_style">Wealth, education, and reasoning style</span></h3>
<p>Various studies have reported positive correlations between levels of education, wealth and <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_quotient" title="Intelligence quotient">IQ</a> with atheism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VyseSI_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-VyseSI-287">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeggelLiveScience_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-GeggelLiveScience-288">[286]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intmag_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-intmag-289">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zuckerman_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zuckerman-125">[123]</a></sup> In a 2008 study, researchers found intelligence to be negatively related to religious belief in Europe and the United States. In a sample of 137 countries, the correlation between national IQ and disbelief in God was found to be 0.60.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intmag_289-1"><a href="#cite_note-intmag-289">[287]</a></sup> According to evolutionary psychologist <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Barber" title="Nigel Barber">Nigel Barber</a>, atheism blossoms in places where most people feel economically secure, particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democracies</a> of Europe, as there is less uncertainty about the future with extensive social safety nets and better health care resulting in a greater quality of life and higher life expectancy. By contrast, in underdeveloped countries, there are far fewer atheists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[288]</a></sup>
</p><p>The relationship between atheism and IQ, while statistically significant, is not a large one, and the reason for the relationship is not well understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VyseSI_287-1"><a href="#cite_note-VyseSI-287">[285]</a></sup> One hypothesis is that the negative relationship between IQ and religiosity is mediated by individual differences in nonconformity; in many countries, religious belief is a conformist choice, and there is evidence that more intelligent people are less likely to conform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[289]</a></sup> Another theory is that people of higher IQ are more likely to engage in analytical reasoning, and that disbelief in religion results from the application of higher-level analytical reasoning to the assessment of religious claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VyseSI_287-2"><a href="#cite_note-VyseSI-287">[285]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a 2017 study, it was shown that compared to religious individuals, atheists have higher reasoning capacities and this difference seemed to be unrelated to sociodemographic factors such as age, education and country of origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[290]</a></sup> In a 2015 study, researchers found that atheists score higher on cognitive reflection tests than theists, the authors wrote that "The fact that atheists score higher agrees with the literature showing that belief is an automatic manifestation of the mind and its default mode. Disbelieving seems to require deliberative cognitive ability."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[291]</a></sup> A 2016 study, in which 4 new studies were reported and a meta-analysis of all previous research on the topic was performed, found that self-identified atheists scored 18.7% higher than theists on the cognitive reflection test and there is a negative correlation between religiosity and analytical thinking. The authors note that recently "it has been argued that analytic thinkers are not actually less religious; rather, the putative association may be a result of religiosity typically being measured after analytic thinking (an order effect)," however, they state "Our results indicate that the association between analytical thinking and religious disbelief is not caused by a simple order effect. There is good evidence that atheists and agnostics are more reflective than religious believers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[292]</a></sup> This "analytic atheist" effect has also been found among academic philosophers, even when controlling for about a dozen potential confounds such as education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[293]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, some studies do not detect this correlation between atheism and analytic thinking in all of the countries that they study,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[294]</a></sup> suggesting that the relationship between analytic thinking and atheism may depend on culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[295]</a></sup> There is also evidence that gender may be involved in the so-called analytic atheist effect: because men have been found more likely to endorse atheism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[296]</a></sup> and men often perform slightly better on tests of analytic thinking<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[297]</a></sup> when not controlling for variables such as math anxiety,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[298]</a></sup> the correlation between atheism and analytic reasoning may be partly explained by whatever explains observed gender differences in analytic thinking.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attitudes_toward_atheism">Attitudes toward atheism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Discrimination_against_atheists" title="Discrimination against atheists">Discrimination against atheists</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blasphemy_laws_worldwide.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1398" data-file-width="2754" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Blasphemy_laws_worldwide.svg/220px-Blasphemy_laws_worldwide.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Blasphemy_laws_worldwide.svg/330px-Blasphemy_laws_worldwide.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Blasphemy_laws_worldwide.svg/440px-Blasphemy_laws_worldwide.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blasphemy_laws_worldwide.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries with punishment for blasphemy. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#37c837; color:black;"> </span> Repealed</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#f9dc36; color:black;"> </span> Local restrictions</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ec8028; color:black;"> </span> Fines and restrictions</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#e73e21; color:black;"> </span> Prison sentences</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#800000; color:white;"> </span> Death sentences</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:States_with_death_penalty_for_apostasy.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2280" data-file-width="4494" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/States_with_death_penalty_for_apostasy.png/220px-States_with_death_penalty_for_apostasy.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/States_with_death_penalty_for_apostasy.png/330px-States_with_death_penalty_for_apostasy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/States_with_death_penalty_for_apostasy.png/440px-States_with_death_penalty_for_apostasy.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:States_with_death_penalty_for_apostasy.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries with the death penalty for <a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">apostasy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[299]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Statistically, atheists are held in poor regard across the globe. Non-atheists, and possibly even fellow atheists, seem to implicitly view atheists as prone to exhibit immoral behaviors ranging from mass murder to not paying at a restaurant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[300]</a></sup> In addition, according to a 2016 <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> publication, 15% of French people, 45% of Americans, and 99% of Indonesians explicitly believe that a person must believe in God to be moral. Pew furthermore noted that, in a U.S. poll, atheists and Muslims tied for the lowest rating among the major religious demographics on a "<a href="/wiki/Feeling_thermometer" title="Feeling thermometer">feeling thermometer</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[301]</a></sup> Also, a study of religious college students found that they were more likely to perceive and interact with atheists negatively after considering their mortality, suggesting that these attitudes may be the result of <a href="/wiki/Death_anxiety#Religiosity" title="Death anxiety">death anxiety</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[302]</a></sup>
</p>
